Note that the players know all previous numbers.

We need to find λ such that Alice has a winning strategy, and λ such that Bazza has a winning strategy.

We can try to analyze the game as a "pursuit-evasion" where each player tries to push the other's cumulative sum over the threshold.

Consider the following: At an odd turn n, Alice can choose x_n to be any number from 0 up to λ n - S_{n-1}. She wants to make it hard for Bazza later. Since Bazza's constraint is Q_n ≤ n for even n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 (if she chooses x_n), she can increase Q_n. But note that after her move, the next even turn is n+1, and Bazza must satisfy Q_n + x_{n+1}^2 ≤ n+1. So if she can make Q_n > n+1, then Bazza loses immediately on his next turn. So she would like to make Q_n > n+1. However, she is limited by S_n ≤ λ n. So the maximum Q_n she can achieve given S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1} is by setting x_n as large as possible, i.e., x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. Then Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. So she can achieve a certain Q_n. She might not want to maximize Q_n, but she might want to ensure that after her move, Q_n > n+1. That requires (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1 - Q_{n-1}. Since Q_{n-1} is the sum of squares of previous numbers.

Similarly, at an even turn n, Bazza can choose x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) to achieve Q_n = n, which maximizes S_n? Actually his move increases S_n by x_n, and Q_n becomes n. After his move, the next odd turn is n+1, and Alice must satisfy S_n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). So if he can make S_n > λ (n+1), then Alice loses on her next turn. So he wants to achieve S_n > λ (n+1). He can choose x_n as large as possible, i.e., x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}), which gives the maximum increase in S_n. So his best chance to push S_n over λ (n+1) is to set x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}), giving S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Then he wants S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1).

Thus the game reduces to the players controlling the evolution of (S_n, Q_n) with the ability to increase one of them at their turn.

This is reminiscent of a "competitive" inequality where the players try to maintain certain relationships. Possibly we can find a "critical" λ by considering the "optimal" strategies that try to keep the game going. The game can be seen as a "battle" between the sum and sum of squares constraints. Since the players have opposite goals, we can think of the game as a "tug-of-war" where the "potential" function f(S, Q, n) = S^2 - n Q? Actually we want to find conditions such that one player can force the other's cumulative sum to exceed the bound.

Let's try to find a strategy for Bazza to force a win for small λ. Suppose λ is very small, say λ < 1. Then Alice's constraint is S_n ≤ λ n. For odd n, after her move, S_n is at most λ n. But note that the sum of squares constraint on even n is Q_n ≤ n. Since S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n, we have S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ sqrt(n * n) = n. So S_n ≤ n. But if λ < 1, then λ n < n, so her constraint is tighter. She may try to keep S_n small, which might help Bazza? Actually Bazza wants to make S_n large. If she keeps S_n small, it's easier for Bazza to increase S_n later? But Bazza can only increase S_n by choosing x_n, and his maximum increase is limited by Q_{n-1}. If S_n is small, then he can potentially increase it by a larger amount because his bound n - Q_{n-1} might be large if Q_{n-1} is small. But Q_{n-1} includes her previous moves; if she chooses small numbers, Q_{n-1} is small, giving him more room to increase S_n. So if λ is small, Alice is forced to keep the sum small, which might actually help Bazza. So maybe Bazza can win for λ < some threshold.

Conversely, if λ is large, say λ > 1, Alice can choose numbers that make Q_n large, causing Bazza to lose. But she must also keep S_n ≤ λ n, which is easy if λ is large. So maybe she can win for λ > some threshold.

But we need to find the exact λ.

Let's attempt to find a "strategy" for Alice to win for λ > 1. She can try to set x_n = 1 on each odd turn? But if λ > 1, then S_n = (n+1)/2 if n odd? Actually with x1=1, S1=1 ≤ λ*1 (since λ>1). Then on turn 2, Bazza might choose x2=0 to keep Q2 ≤ 2. Then on turn 3, Alice sets x3=1, S3=2 ≤ 3λ (fine). Q3 = 1+0+1=2. Then on turn 4, Bazza chooses x4 such that Q4 ≤ 4. If he chooses x4 = sqrt(4-2)=sqrt(2)≈1.414, then S4 = 2+1.414=3.414. Then on turn 5, Alice must choose x5 such that S5 ≤ 5λ. If λ>1, 5λ >5, so S5=3.414+ x5 ≤5λ is easy. She could set x5=1, S5=4.414, Q5=2+1+2=5? Actually Q5 = Q4 + x5^2 = 4 + 1 =5. Then turn 6: Bazza needs Q6 ≤6, Q5=5, so he can choose x6 = sqrt(6-5)=1, S6=4.414+1=5.414. Then turn 7: Alice needs S7 ≤7λ, etc. This seems to continue indefinitely; but does Bazza ever lose? He can always set x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}), which gives Q_n = n. Then the maximum S_n he can achieve is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). If Q_{n-1} is exactly n-1? Actually after Alice's move, Q_n is less than or equal to n? But she could increase Q_n. In this naive strategy, Alice always picks 1, and Bazza picks max to make Q_n = n. Then S_n after his move is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Since Q_{n-1} = (n-1) after his previous move? Let's simulate: Turn 1: Alice x1=1, S1=1, Q1=1. Turn 2: Bazza sets x2 = sqrt(2-1)=1, S2=2, Q2=2. Turn 3: Alice x3=1, S3=3, Q3=3. Turn 4: Bazza x4 = sqrt(4-3)=1, S4=4, Q4=4. So if both players always choose 1 on their turns, then we get S_n = n, Q_n = n. This satisfies both constraints: For odd n, S_n = n ≤ λ n, so λ ≥ 1. For even n, Q_n = n ≤ n, so equality. This is a valid play. Then the game goes on forever (since both players can always choose 1). But is this a winning strategy for Alice? No, because she is not forcing Bazza to lose; they both are playing in a way that the game never ends. But she might have a different strategy that forces Bazza to eventually be unable to move. For λ > 1, she could try to increase Q_n faster than Bazza can keep up? For instance, she could choose x_n = c (some constant >1) on odd turns. Then S_n would increase by c each odd turn. But her sum constraint: S_n ≤ λ n. If she chooses c > λ? That would violate her constraint because S_n would eventually exceed λ n. So she must choose x_n such that S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. If λ is large, she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. To make Q_n large, she wants to maximize x_n, but then S_n also becomes larger. However, she can choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum allowed). That would make S_n = λ n exactly, and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. That could potentially cause Q_n to become very large. But then Bazza on his next turn must satisfy Q_n + x_{n+1}^2 ≤ n+1. If Q_n is huge, then he cannot choose any x_{n+1} (even 0) because Q_n > n+1. So if Alice can make Q_n > n+1 at some odd turn n, then Bazza loses immediately. So she wants to achieve Q_n > n+1.

Thus the problem reduces to: For a given λ, can Alice force that at some odd turn n, the sum of squares of the numbers chosen so far (including her move) exceeds n+1? She can control the numbers on odd turns, but Bazza controls on even turns. She can try to push Q_n over the threshold. However, Bazza might try to keep Q_n small by choosing small x_n (like zero) on even turns, which would keep Q_n low, but he also wants to eventually make S_n > λ (n+1) to force Alice to lose. So there is a competition.

We need to find a "critical" λ such that if λ is above that value, Alice can force a win; if λ is below that value, Bazza can force a win. Possibly the critical λ is the positive solution to some equation derived from the "optimal" strategies that maintain the game indefinitely.

Consider the following: Suppose both players play "optimally" to avoid losing. That is, they try to keep the game going. For the game to go on indefinitely, the sequences must satisfy the constraints for all n. So there exist sequences x_n such that for all n, S_n ≤ λ n for odd n and Q_n ≤ n for even n. For a given λ, what is the condition for existence of an infinite sequence? Possibly the condition is λ ≥ 1? Because if λ < 1, then at odd n, S_n ≤ λ n < n, but then Q_n ≤ n from even turns; however, by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n, so S_n ≤ sqrt(n Q_n) ≤ sqrt(n * n) = n. That doesn't give a contradiction. So the existence of a draw strategy is trivial: both players can choose zero always. But the problem asks: determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. That suggests that for some λ, one player can force a win despite the opponent's zero strategy? Actually if the opponent plays zero, the player can choose numbers to try to force a win. Similarly, Bazza's winning strategy must work even if Alice chooses zeros.

So the "zero strategy" is a specific strategy for the opponent. The players are trying to force a win, so they will play optimally against the opponent's strategy. If one player has a winning strategy, that strategy must guarantee a win against any opponent play, including the zero play. So we need to analyze whether Alice can force a win when Bazza plays zero, and whether Bazza can force a win when Alice plays zero. The problem is to find λ for which such a strategy exists.

Thus we need to find the "critical" λ where the game transitions from Bazza having a winning strategy to Alice having a winning strategy. Possibly the critical λ is 1, but we need to test.

Consider λ > 1. Can Alice force a win? Suppose she tries to set x_n = 1 on each odd turn. Then Bazza, if he plays zero, then S_n after odd n is (n+1)/2, and Q_n = (n+1)/2. Let's analyze the dynamics if Alice uses a "maximal" strategy: On each odd turn n, she chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (so S_n = λ n). On even turns, Bazza can choose any x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_n ≤ n. Actually note that for any sequence, the quantity D_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 is nonnegative. It measures "variance". If the players play to avoid losing, they might try to keep D_n bounded. Let's compute D_n evolution.

Suppose at turn n, the state is (S, Q). The player can choose x_n such that the new state is (S+x_n, Q+x_n^2). Then D_{new} = (n+1)Q_{new} - S_{new}^2 = (n+1)(Q+x_n^2) - (S+x_n)^2 = (n+1)Q + (n+1)x_n^2 - S^2 - 2Sx_n - x_n^2 = (nQ - S^2) + (n+1)x_n^2 - 2Sx_n - x_n^2 = D_n + (n)x_n^2 - 2Sx_n = D_n + n x_n^2 - 2S x_n.

So D_{new} = D_n + x_n (n x_n - 2S). This is a quadratic expression.

Thus we need to analyze the game with both players playing optimally. This is a typical "alternating" game where each player tries to maximize their own "resource" while minimizing the opponent's. We can try to characterize the "winning" region by considering the "critical" sequences that maintain the game indefinitely.

Consider the following: For the game to go on forever, there must exist infinite sequences x_n such that for all n, S_n ≤ λ n for odd n and Q_n ≤ n for even n. Since the players can always choose zero, they can always keep the game going. But the question is whether one player can force a win. For a player to have a winning strategy, they must be able to "break" the other's ability to continue indefinitely. That is, they can force a situation where the other player has no legal move.

One common approach in such games is to consider a "potential" function that is monotone and bounded, and the player who can push it over a threshold wins. Another approach is to use "strategy stealing" or "mirror" strategies.

Given the form of the constraints, we might guess that the threshold is λ = 1. But earlier we found that for λ > 1/√2, Alice can win by playing the maximal strategy (choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} on odd turns). However, we need to ensure that in that strategy, she can always make a move. That requires S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. If she is playing maximal, she is making S_n = λ n on her turns. But after Bazza's move, S_n could be larger than λ n? Actually Bazza's move increases S_n. If Bazza chooses a positive x_n, then S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n. Since S_{n-1} = λ (n-1) (odd turn), and he chooses x_n = something, then S_n could be > λ n. That would cause Alice to lose on her next turn because she would need S_n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1) and S_n is already > λ n? Let's examine: Suppose at turn n-1 (odd), Alice sets x_{n-1} = λ (n-1) - S_{n-2} (so S_{n-1}=λ(n-1)). Then Bazza at turn n (even) chooses x_n. He can choose any x_n from 0 to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). So Bazza would like to make x_n > 2λ. So if λ is small, he can achieve that; if λ is large, maybe not. In the maximal strategy for Alice, she wants to keep S_n as low as possible to avoid losing. But she also wants to make Q_n large. So she might need to choose x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} on her turns, but that might leave her vulnerable to Bazza's large x_n. However, she could choose a smaller x_n to keep S_n low, but then Q_n doesn't grow as fast. So there is a trade-off.

Thus we need to find a strategy for Alice that guarantees she can eventually force Q_n over n+1, while also ensuring that Bazza cannot make S_n exceed λ (n+1) on an even turn. So Alice needs to "defend" her own sum constraint.

Perhaps a better approach: Consider the following "critical" strategy: Alice aims to maintain the invariant S_n^2 = n Q_n (i.e., equality in Cauchy-Schwarz). That would imply that all x_i are equal? Actually if S_n^2 = n Q_n, then all x_i are equal (by equality condition of Cauchy-Schwarz). So if she can keep the numbers equal, then the constraints might be balanced. The constraints: For odd n, S_n ≤ λ n => n c ≤ λ n => c ≤ λ. For even n, Q_n ≤ n => n c^2 ≤ n => c^2 ≤ 1 => c ≤ 1. So if λ ≥ 1, then c=1 works? Actually if λ≥1, then c=1 satisfies c≤λ and c≤1, so the game can go on forever with x_n=1 for all n. That is a draw. If λ<1, then c=1 violates the sum constraint? Actually if λ<1, then c=1 gives S_n = n > λ n, so Alice cannot choose 1 on odd turns. So the equal numbers strategy is only possible if λ≥1. For λ<1, the equal numbers strategy is not legal because Alice cannot choose 1 on odd turns. So for λ<1, perhaps Bazza can force a win by making the sum large? But we already saw that for λ=0.6, Bazza can win. For λ=0.9 (<1), is Bazza still winning? Let's test λ=0.9. Maybe we can solve the game by considering the "optimal" sequences that keep the game going indefinitely. For the game to be a draw (no winning strategy), the players must have strategies to avoid losing indefinitely. This is like a "non-terminating" play. Typically, in such games, the critical λ is where the "maximal" strategy for one player is "safe" (i.e., the other cannot force a win). This is reminiscent of a "tug-of-war" where each player can "defend" their own constraint by playing the "mirror" strategy.

We can attempt to find a "strategy" for Bazza that prevents Alice from ever making Q_n > n+1 at an odd turn, for λ < 1/√2. And similarly, a strategy for Alice that prevents Bazza from ever making S_n > λ(n+1) at an even turn, for λ > 1/√2. The threshold likely is λ = 1/√2.

But we need to prove that if λ > 1/√2, Alice can force a win. Let's try to construct a concrete winning strategy for Alice.

Idea: Alice can aim to "control" the quantity Q_n - n (the surplus over the bound). She can try to make Q_n exceed n+1 at some odd n. She can do this by choosing x_n sufficiently large. However, she is limited by S_n ≤ λ n. To maximize Q_n, she should choose the maximum possible x_n on each odd turn. But as we saw, if she does that, S_n becomes λ n exactly, and then Bazza can choose x_n to increase S_n, which might cause Alice to lose later. But she can also choose a smaller x_n to keep S_n low. However, if she chooses a smaller x_n, Q_n grows slower. So she needs a balance.

Perhaps a better strategy: Alice can adopt a "constant" strategy: She chooses x_n = c (a constant) on all odd turns. She can choose c to be as large as possible while still satisfying the sum constraint? Actually the sum constraint is S_n ≤ λ n for odd n. If she sets x_n = c on odd turns, then after odd turn n, S_n = (n+1)/2 * c? Because she contributes c on each odd turn, and Bazza contributes some numbers on even turns. But she doesn't control Bazza's numbers. To ensure she can always move, she needs S_{n-1} ≤ λ n for odd n. If Bazza chooses large numbers, S_{n-1} might become too large. So she might need to limit Bazza's ability to increase S_n. She can do this by keeping her own S_n small, but if she chooses a constant c, S_n will increase over time. She could choose c = 1 (if λ≥1) or c = λ? Actually if λ < 1, she cannot choose c=1 because then S_n would exceed λ n eventually. So she must choose c ≤ λ. But she also wants to make Q_n large. The maximum Q_n she can achieve per turn is by setting x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. That could be larger than a constant. So perhaps a "greedy" strategy of always choosing the maximum possible x_n on her turns is optimal for her to make Q_n grow as fast as possible. But we need to ensure that she can always do that (i.e., the move is legal) and that she will not be forced to lose earlier. The issue is that after Bazza's move, S_n might become too large, causing her to lose on her next turn. So she must ensure that Bazza cannot make S_n > λ (n+1). She can try to "defend" by making S_n as small as possible, i.e., choosing x_n = 0 on her turns. But then Q_n doesn't increase. So there is a trade-off.

Maybe the critical λ is where the "maximal" strategy for Alice leads to a "safe" state where Bazza cannot win. Let's analyze the recurrence if both players play "maximal" in their own interest: Alice always chooses the maximum allowed on her turns, Bazza always chooses the maximum allowed on his turns. But what is Bazza's objective? He wants to win. If he is playing to win, he might not always choose the maximal x_n; he might choose a smaller x_n to avoid giving Alice a chance to win. So we need to consider optimal strategies.

In such "alternating" games, often the winning region is determined by the "critical" sequence where the players are playing "optimally" to maintain the game indefinitely. That is, we can look for a "non-terminating" strategy for both players that avoids losing. If such a strategy exists, then neither player can force a win. If for some λ, one player can deviate to force a win, then that player has a winning strategy. So we need to find the set of λ for which there exists a strategy for one player that guarantees a win regardless of the opponent's play.

A common method is to consider "potential" functions that are monotone and bounded, and the player who can increase it beyond a certain bound wins. For example, define V_n = S_n^2 - n Q_n. This is ≤ 0. Perhaps the condition for Bazza to win is that he can keep V_n negative enough? Actually if V_n becomes too negative, it might mean that the sum of squares is large relative to the sum, which could be beneficial for Alice? Not sure.

Another approach: Consider the game as a "battle" between two inequalities. We can try to find a "strategy" for Bazza that ensures S_n stays bounded by something, preventing Alice from ever winning, for λ less than some threshold. Similarly, a strategy for Alice that ensures Q_n stays bounded by something, preventing Bazza from ever winning, for λ greater than some threshold. The threshold might be the solution to λ = 1/√2.

Now, we need to prove that for λ > 1/√2, Alice wins. The strategy: On each odd turn, she chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. That is, she chooses the maximum allowed. We need to show that this move is always legal (i.e., λ n - S_{n-1} ≥ 0) and that she never loses (i.e., she can always make a move). So she adds 2λ each odd turn. Then Q_{n+1} = Q_n + (2λ)^2. So Q after odd turns grows as Q_{2m+1} = (4m+1)λ^2 (starting from Q_1 = λ^2). Then we need to show that for λ^2 > 1/2, there exists an odd n such that Q_n > n+1. This is equivalent to (4m+1)λ^2 > 2m+2. As argued, if λ^2 > 1/2, then the inequality holds for sufficiently large m. So eventually, after her move at odd n, Q_n > n+1. Then on the next even turn (n+1), Bazza must choose x_{n+1} such that Q_{n+1} ≤ n+1. But Q_n > n+1, so even with x_{n+1}=0, the inequality fails. So Bazza loses. So this strategy works for λ > 1/√2, provided that Bazza always chooses x_n=0 on his turns. But what if Bazza chooses a nonzero x_n? That would only increase Q_n even faster, which helps Alice. So his best chance to avoid losing is to set x_n=0. So the strategy is winning for Alice.

Now, we need to verify that the move x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} is always nonnegative. For λ > 1/√2, we need to show that S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. In our strategy, after her move at odd n-1, S_{n-1}=λ(n-1). So S_{n-1}=λ(n-1) ≤ λ n holds. Then Bazza's move at even n: he sets x_n=0, so S_n=λ(n-1). Then on her next turn n+1, she needs x_{n+1}=λ(n+1)-S_n = λ(n+1)-λ(n-1)=2λ, which is positive. So she can always make the move. So the strategy is feasible. Thus for λ > 1/√2, Alice has a winning strategy.

For λ = 1/√2, we need to show that neither player has a winning strategy. That is, the game is a draw. We already have a draw strategy: Both players play in a way that the game never ends. For example, Alice always chooses x_n = 0 on odd turns, Bazza always chooses x_n = √2 on even turns. But we must check that this is feasible: For n=1, Alice chooses x1=0, S1=0, Q1=0. Then n=2, Bazza chooses x2 = √(2 - Q1)=√2, S2=√2, Q2=2. n=3, Alice must choose x3 such that S2+x3 ≤ 3λ = 3/√2. S2=√2≈1.414, so x3 ≤ 3/√2 - √2 = (3-2)/√2 = 1/√2≈0.7071. She chooses x3=0 (or any ≤0.7071). If she chooses 0, then S3=√2, Q3=2. Then n=4, Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3)=√2, S4=√2+√2=2√2, Q4=4. Then n=5, Alice bound = 5/√2≈3.5355, S4=2√2≈2.828, so x5 ≤ 3.5355-2.828=0.7075, she chooses 0, S5=2.828, Q5=4. Then n=6, Bazza x6=√(6-4)=√2, S6=2.828+1.414=4.242, Q6=6. n=7, bound=7/√2≈4.9497, S6=4.242, x7≤0.7077, she chooses 0, S7=4.242, Q7=6. Then n=8, Bazza x8=√(8-6)=√2, S8=4.242+1.414=5.656, Q8=8. And so on. So indeed, the game continues forever. So there is no winning strategy for either. But we must also show that no player can force a win. For Alice, can she deviate to win? She might try to choose a positive x_n to make Q_n > n+1. But as we saw earlier, if she chooses a positive x_n, she must keep S_n ≤ λ n. With λ=1/√2, if she tries to increase Q, she might cause S to become too large for her to move later. Let's analyze if she can win. Suppose at turn 1, she chooses x1=1 (max allowed is 0.7071, so she cannot choose 1). So she is limited to at most 0.7071. If she chooses x1=0.7071, then S1=0.7071, Q1=0.5. Then n=2: Bazza chooses x2 = √(2-0.5)=√1.5=1.2247. S2=0.7071+1.2247=1.9318, Q2=2. Then n=3: bound=3*0.7071=2.1213, S2=1.9318, so x3 ≤ 0.1895. Then n=7: bound=7*0.7071=4.9497, S6=4.9497, x7≤0, so she must choose x7=0. So she cannot win because Q6=6, n+1=8? Actually after n=6, Q6=6, then at n=7, she can only choose x7=0, Q7=6, n+1=8, so not >8. So she doesn't win. If she tries to choose a larger x3, she can't because bound is 0.1895. So she cannot force a win. If she tries to choose x1=0, then we already have the draw. So no winning strategy for Alice. For Bazza, can he win? He could try to set x2=0 (instead of sqrt(2)) but then Alice at n=3 could choose x3=2.1213 and win? Actually if x1=0, and Bazza chooses x2=0, then S2=0, Q2=0. Then Alice at n=3: bound=2.1213, she can choose x3=2.1213, S3=2.1213, Q3=4.5. Then n=4: Bazza must choose x4 such that Q4 ≤4. But Q3=4.5>4, so he cannot move. So he loses. So he must set x2=√2. So the draw is the only way to avoid loss. So for λ=1/√2, the game is a draw.

Thus the answer: Alice has a winning strategy for λ > 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy for λ < 1/√2, and the game is a draw for λ = 1/√2.

But we need to present a rigorous proof. We need to justify that for λ < 1/√2, Bazza can force a win, and for λ > 1/√2, Alice can force a win.

Since the right-hand side increases to 1/√2, for any λ < 1/√2, we can find m such that (m√2)/(2m+1) > λ. Indeed, the sequence a_m = m√2/(2m+1) is increasing and tends to 1/√2. So for λ < 1/√2, there exists M such that a_M > λ. Then at m=M, T_M = M√2 > λ(2M+1), so Alice loses at her next turn. So Bazza wins. If Alice chooses any positive x, T_m will be even larger, so the condition holds even earlier.

Thus Bazza has a winning strategy for λ < 1/√2.

Now, we need to present the proof in a rigorous way.

We also need to consider the case λ = 1/√2. We need to show that neither player has a winning strategy. We already have a draw strategy: For Alice, always choose x_n = 0 on odd turns. For Bazza, always choose x_n = √2 on even turns. But we need to verify that this is feasible. Check: For n=1, x1=0 gives S1=0, Q1=0. n=2, x2=√2 gives S2=√2, Q2=2. n=3, Alice must choose x3 such that S2+x3 ≤ 3λ = 3/√2. S2=√2, so x3 ≤ 3/√2 - √2 = 1/√2. She can choose x3=0. So it's feasible. Then n=4, Bazza chooses x4 = √(4 - Q3). Q3 = 2 (since x3=0), so x4=√2. S4=√2+√2=2√2, Q4=4. n=5, Alice bound = 5/√2, S4=2√2, so x5 ≤ 5/√2 - 2√2 = (5-4)/√2 = 1/√2, she chooses 0. So the game continues indefinitely. So there is no winning strategy for either. However, we must also argue that no player can force a win. For Alice, could she deviate to win? She would need to make Q exceed n+1 at some odd turn. But as we argued, the maximum x she can choose is limited by the bound λ n - S_{n-1}. With λ=1/√2, and if she chooses a positive x, S_{n-1} might become larger, but we need to show that she cannot achieve x > √2. Since the bound for her move is λ (2m+1) - T_m, and T_m ≥ m√2 (as we can prove from the recurrence), we have λ(2m+1)-T_m ≤ (2m+1)/√2 - m√2 = (2m+1 - 2m)/√2 = 1/√2 < √2. So her x_m is at most 1/√2. Thus she can never make Q > n+1. So she cannot win. For Bazza, he could try to deviate from the maximal move. If he chooses x2=0, then Alice at turn 3 can choose x3=2.1213 (since bound=2.1213) and win. So that's not winning for him. If he chooses any x2 < √2, then Alice can choose x3=λ*3 - (x1+x2). She might be able to win by making Q large. But we need to show that Bazza cannot force a win. The existence of a draw strategy shows that neither has a winning strategy. But we must also argue that Bazza cannot force a win because there is a strategy for Alice to avoid losing (the zero strategy). So it's a draw.

Thus the answer: Alice wins for λ > 1/√2, Bazza wins for λ < 1/√2, and the game is a draw for λ = 1/√2.

Let's reconsider the game more generally. The players are trying to force the opponent to be unable to move. For Alice to win, she needs to ensure that at some even turn n, Q_{n-1} > n. Since she controls odd turns, she can try to increase Q. Bazza controls even turns, and he can try to make S_{n-1} > λ n to force her to lose. So it's a race. The critical λ might be where the "optimal" play leads to a draw. For λ > 1/√2, maybe Alice can force a win by playing a "constant" strategy? Let's try to see if there is a strategy for Alice that guarantees Q_n > n+1 at some odd n, regardless of Bazza's moves. We can try to analyze the game using "potential" functions that are monotone and bounded.

Define after each of Alice's moves (odd n), consider the quantity R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2. This is nonnegative. We can compute its evolution. On her turn at odd n, she chooses x_n such that S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. The new state: S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n, Q_n = Q_{n-1}+x_n^2. So R_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 = n(Q_{n-1}+x_n^2) - (S_{n-1}+x_n)^2 = n Q_{n-1} + n x_n^2 - S_{n-1}^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n - x_n^2 = (n Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2) + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n. But note that n Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2 is not simply R_{n-1} because R_{n-1} = (n-1) Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2. So R_n = R_{n-1} + Q_{n-1} + (n-1)x_n^2 - 2 S_{n-1} x_n. This is messy.

Alternatively, consider the quantity T_n = S_n - λ n (the slack for Alice). At the start of her turn at odd n, she needs S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. She can choose x_n to increase S_n. The worst-case for her is when Bazza makes S_{n-1} as large as possible. But Bazza's move at even n-1 increases S_{n-1} by some amount. So if Bazza wants to cause her to lose, he will try to make S_{n-1} > λ n. So to win, Alice must prevent that. So she needs a strategy that ensures that no matter how Bazza chooses his numbers, S_{n-1} never exceeds λ n on her turns. That is, she must keep the sum small. But she also wants to increase Q.

Perhaps the correct strategy for Alice when λ > 1/√2 is to always choose x_n = 0 on her turns? That would keep S low, but Q doesn't increase. So she cannot win. So she needs to increase Q but also keep S low. She can do this by choosing a small x_n that still grows Q. The maximum Q she can achieve per turn is limited by the sum bound. If she chooses x_n = c (a small constant), then S_n grows by c each odd turn, so S_n ≈ c*(n/2) (if Bazza plays zeros). Then the bound λ n will be much larger if λ is large, so she can survive. But then Q_n grows by c^2 each odd turn, so Q_n ≈ c^2*(n/2). For her to win, she needs Q_n > n+1, i.e., c^2*(n/2) > n => c^2 > 2 => c > √2. But she cannot choose c > √2 because then her sum bound would be violated? Actually if she chooses x_n = c, then S_n = S_{n-1}+c. If Bazza plays zeros, S_{2m+1}= (m+1)c. The bound is λ(2m+1). So she needs (m+1)c ≤ λ(2m+1) for all m. For large m, this becomes c ≤ 2λ. Since λ > 1/√2 ≈0.707, 2λ > 1.414, so c could be up to about 1.414. But she also needs c > √2 ≈1.414 to win? Actually c > √2 would give c^2 >2, so Q_n ≈ c^2*m/2 > m, which is > n+1? Let's check: If c=1.5, then Q_n ≈ 2.25*m/2 = 1.125m, and n+1 ≈ 2m+2? Actually n=2m+1, so n+1=2m+2, so Q_n grows as 1.125m, which is much less than 2m+2, so she doesn't win. The condition Q_n > n+1 is (c^2*m/2) > 2m+2 => c^2 > 4 + 4/m, so for large m, c^2 > 4 => c > 2. So she would need c > 2. But then her sum bound would be violated because S_n = (m+1)c ≈ 2m, and λ n ≈ λ(2m)=2λ m. For λ>1/√2, 2λ > √2 ≈1.414, so S_n could be up to 2m if c=2, but λ n = 2λ m, which is about 1.414m? Actually λ n = λ(2m+1) ≈ 2λ m. For λ=0.8, 2λ=1.6, so S_n = 2m is larger than 1.6m, so she would lose. So constant c > 2 is not feasible. Thus she cannot win by picking a constant.

So the only way to make Q_n exceed n+1 is to make x_n large enough, but then the sum constraint becomes tight. The strategy of always picking the maximum allowed (x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}) ensures that S_n = λ n exactly. Then Q_n after her move will be Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. If Bazza chooses zeros, this leads to Q_n = (4m+1)λ^2, which for λ>1/√2 eventually exceeds n+1. But if Bazza chooses positive numbers, S_{n-1} may become larger than λ n? Actually in the simulation, S_{n-1} after his move was less than λ n, so she could still pick x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. However, note that when Bazza chooses positive, S_{n-1} becomes larger, so her x_n becomes smaller, which reduces the increase in Q. So the "maximal" strategy may not guarantee that Q exceeds n+1 because her x_n might be too small. So we need a different strategy for Alice that can handle Bazza's positive moves.

Perhaps the winning strategy for Alice is to always set x_n = 1? But if λ is small, she can't. For λ > 1/√2, maybe she can set x_n = 1 on odd turns? Let's test λ=0.8. If she sets x1=1, S1=1, Q1=1. Then Bazza at turn2 can choose x2 = sqrt(2-1)=1, S2=2, Q2=2. Then Alice at turn3: she needs S3 ≤ 3*0.8=2.4, but S2=2, so she can set x3=0.4, S3=2.4, Q3=2+0.16=2.16. Then Bazza at turn4: x4 = sqrt(4-2.16)= sqrt(1.84)=1.356, S4=2.4+1.356=3.756, Q4=4. Then Alice turn5: bound=4, S4=3.756, x5=0.244, S5=4, Q5=4+0.0595=4.0595. What about setting x_n = λ? For λ=0.8, x_n=0.8. Then S1=0.8, Q1=0.64. Then Bazza x2 = sqrt(2-0.64)=1.28, S2=2.08, Q2=2. Then Alice turn3: she can set x3 = λ*3 - S2 = 2.4-2.08=0.32, S3=2.4, Q3=2+0.1024=2.1024. Then Bazza turn4: x4 = sqrt(4-2.1024)= sqrt(1.8976)=1.377, S4=3.777, Q4=4. Thus the "maximal" strategy for Alice does not seem to guarantee a win against Bazza's positive moves. So maybe the correct analysis is that for λ > 1/√2, Alice can force a win by a different strategy, perhaps one that keeps S small but also increases Q. Let's think about the game as a kind of "race". At each step, Alice can increase Q by some amount, and Bazza can increase S by some amount. The condition for Alice to win is that she can eventually make Q exceed n+1. The condition for Bazza to win is that he can eventually make S exceed λ (n+1). The critical λ is where the "optimal" play leads to a draw. I recall a known result: In a two-player game where one player tries to maximize the sum of squares and the other tries to maximize the sum, the threshold is λ = 1/√2. For λ > 1/√2, the sum-of-squares player can win by playing a "greedy" strategy, but that strategy might be to set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} on her turns. But as we saw, if Bazza plays large numbers, S becomes large, and she might not be able to play that move. However, she can also choose to play small numbers to keep S low. The key is to find a strategy that ensures that Q_n eventually exceeds n+1 regardless of Bazza's play. Perhaps she can adopt a "conservative" strategy: she chooses x_n = 0 on all her turns? That doesn't increase Q. So she must increase Q. She can try to set x_n = a (a small constant) when Bazza is playing zeros, but if Bazza plays positive, S increases, which might cause her to be forced to choose x_n = 0? Actually if Bazza makes S large, she might be forced to choose x_n = 0 because S_{n-1} > λ n? But if S_{n-1} > λ n, she already lost. So she must prevent that. So she must keep S small. So she must choose x_n to be as small as possible to keep S from becoming too large. But then Q grows slowly. So she needs to find a "safe" growth rate for Q. This is reminiscent of the "competitive" inequality: The condition S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n implies that if Q_n is large relative to n, then S_n is also large. So there is a trade-off.

Maybe we should analyze the game by considering the "optimal" strategies for both players that try to keep the game going. For the game to be a draw, the players can play such that the inequalities hold for all n. The "critical" λ is the supremum of λ for which Bazza can force a win? Actually we need to find the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. That is, there exists a strategy for Alice that guarantees a win no matter what Bazza does. This is a typical "winning region" for the player who wants to achieve Q_n > n+1. We need to determine for which λ Alice can force that.

We can approach this using the concept of "strategy stealing" or "potential" functions that are "monotone" and "bounded". For instance, define a function F_n = S_n - λ n. If Alice can keep this non-positive for all her turns, she can continue. But she wants to eventually make Q_n > n+1. Perhaps there is a known "critical" λ = 1/√2 from the inequality: For any sequence, if λ ≥ 1/√2, then there exists a strategy for Alice to win? Let's test numerically with λ=0.8. We tried the maximal strategy and saw that against Bazza's positive play, the game didn't end. Could Alice have chosen a different strategy to win? Suppose she tries to set x1 = 0.8 (max), then as we saw, Bazza can set x2=1.28, and she can set x3=0.32, etc. This seems to lead to a draw. What if she sets x1 = 0.8, then Bazza chooses x2=0.5? Then S2=1.3, Q2=0.64+0.25=0.89. Then Alice at turn3: bound=2.4, S2=1.3, she can choose x3=1.1, S3=2.4, Q3=0.89+1.21=2.1. Maybe she can win by setting x1 = 0.8, then Bazza chooses x2 = 0? Then she can set x3 = 1.6, S3=2.4, Q3=0.64+2.56=3.2. Then turn6: x6 = sqrt(6-4.498)= sqrt(1.502)=1.226, S6=4+1.226=5.226, Q6=6. Then turn7: bound=5.6, S6=5.226, x7=0.374, S7=5.6, Q7=6+0.139=6.139. This seems to continue indefinitely. So not win.

Thus, it appears that for λ=0.8, the game may be a draw if both players play optimally. But earlier we had a strategy that gave Alice a win against Bazza's zero strategy. However, if Bazza chooses a positive strategy, Alice cannot win. So the question is: does Alice have a strategy that guarantees a win regardless of Bazza's play? In the above simulations, we saw that when Bazza played positive, Alice could follow the maximal strategy but the game didn't end. Could Alice deviate to force a win? We need to determine the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy. This means there exists a strategy for Alice that guarantees that eventually Bazza cannot move, regardless of Bazza's moves. So we need to find a strategy that works against all possible Bazza plays.

From the simulations, when λ<1, Bazza can win by playing the maximal strategy (making Q_n = n). That is, he can ensure that Alice never makes Q exceed n+1 because her x is limited. So for λ<1, Bazza has a winning strategy. When λ=1, we saw that if both play optimally (Bazza playing maximal), the game is a draw. But can Alice force a win for λ=1? She would need a strategy that works against any Bazza. If Bazza plays the maximal strategy, the game is a draw. So she cannot force a win. So λ=1 is a draw. When λ>1, we saw that if she plays the maximal strategy, she wins because she can make Q exceed n+1 at some odd turn before Bazza can cause her to lose. But is it possible for Bazza to avoid losing by playing a different strategy? For λ>1, we need to check if Bazza can force a draw. In the simulation for λ=1.2, we saw that with Bazza playing positive, Alice still won. But could Bazza choose a different strategy to avoid losing? For instance, he might try to keep Q low by not making his moves too large, but then Alice's x might be larger. The key is that for λ>1, the recurrence might lead to a situation where no matter what Bazza does, Alice can eventually cause Q to exceed n+1. But we need to prove that.

We can try to analyze the game using a "potential" function that Alice can control. For instance, consider the quantity \(P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2\). This is nonnegative. Under Alice's optimal play, she wants to increase P_n. On her turn, she chooses x_n. The new P_{n+1} = (n+1)Q_{n+1} - S_{n+1}^2 = (n+1)(Q_n + x_n^2) - (S_n + x_n)^2 = nQ_n - S_n^2 + (n+1)x_n^2 - 2S_n x_n - x_n^2 = P_n + n x_n^2 - 2S_n x_n. So P_{n+1} - P_n = x_n (n x_n - 2S_n). For odd n, Alice chooses x_n. She wants to make this as large as possible. The maximum possible x_n is λ n - S_{n-1}. If she chooses that, then the increase is something. If she chooses a smaller x, the increase might be smaller. So to increase P quickly, she should choose the maximum allowed. That seems optimal.

Now, we also have the constraint that on even turns, Bazza chooses x_n such that Q_n ≤ n. He can choose any x_n from 0 to √(n - Q_{n-1}). His goal is to minimize P_n or prevent Alice from making it too large. Since P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2, and Q_n = n at his move (if he chooses max), then P_n = n^2 - S_n^2. So P_n is the "slack" of S relative to n. If he chooses a smaller x, Q_n < n, then P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2, which is smaller? Actually if he chooses x smaller, Q_n is smaller, so P_n = n Q_n - S_n^2 is smaller than if he chose max (since Q_n is smaller, and S_n is smaller as well? S_n = S_{n-1}+x_n, so if x_n is smaller, S_n is smaller, and the term n Q_n - S_n^2 could be larger or smaller). Let's compute: If he chooses x = 0, then Q_n = Q_{n-1}, S_n = S_{n-1}. Then P_n = n Q_{n-1} - S_{n-1}^2. Since n > n-1, this could be larger than P_{n-1}? Not sure.

But we need to be rigorous. Let's prove:

1. If λ > 1, Alice can force a win by always choosing the maximum allowed: x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} on odd turns. We need to show that this move is always legal and that eventually Bazza cannot move. We need to argue that under this strategy, Alice never loses (i.e., S_{n-1} ≤ λ n for her turns). This is true because after her move at odd n, S_n = λ n. Then Bazza's move at even n+1 adds some x_{n+1} ≥ 0, so S_{n+1} ≥ λ n. Then on her next turn (n+2), she needs S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+2). Since S_{n+1} = λ n + x_{n+1}, we have λ n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+2) => x_{n+1} ≤ 2λ. But x_{n+1} could be large? However, if x_{n+1} > 2λ, then S_{n+1} > λ n + 2λ = λ (n+2), so she would not have a move at turn n+2. But could that happen? Bazza chooses x_{n+1} to maximize his chance to win. He can set x_{n+1} = √(n+1 - Q_n) (max). But we need to show that in the maximal strategy for Alice, she can still survive and eventually cause Q to exceed n+1. The key is that for λ > 1, the recurrence leads to a point where Q becomes so large that Bazza cannot move. Let's analyze the recurrence more generally.

We need to show that if λ > 1, then regardless of Bazza's choices, Alice's maximal strategy will eventually cause Q to exceed n+1 at an odd turn. Actually we need to prove that there exists some m such that after her move at odd 2m+1, Q_{2m+1} > 2m+2. Since she is playing x_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1) - S_{2m}. But S_{2m} depends on previous moves. We need to bound S_{2m} from below. Since Bazza can make S increase, S_{2m} might be large. However, if Bazza makes S too large, Alice will be unable to move at her turn, which would be a win for Bazza. So she must prevent that. So the strategy must be such that she can always move. But is it always possible for her to move if she plays the maximal strategy? That depends on whether S_{2m} ≤ λ(2m+1). Since she chooses x_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1) - S_{2m}, this is nonnegative only if S_{2m} ≤ λ(2m+1). So she must ensure that Bazza's moves do not cause S_{2m} to exceed λ(2m+1). But she can also choose a different move if needed. Perhaps the winning strategy for Alice is not the maximal strategy, but a different one that ensures S stays low enough. For λ > 1, she could adopt a "conservative" strategy: set x_n = 0 on some turns to keep S low, but then Q doesn't grow. However, she can also try to set x_n = something like 1? But we need a strategy that guarantees a win. Let's try to find a general approach.

Maybe we can treat the game as a "pursuit" where Alice wants to achieve Q_n > n+1. The condition for her to win is that she can force the inequality \(n Q_n - S_n^2\) to become large enough. Since she controls odd turns, she can increase this potential by choosing x_n large. However, Bazza can also increase S and Q. We need to analyze the "critical" λ where the game is balanced.

We can look for a "strategy" that maintains the invariant \(S_n = \lambda n\) for odd n. That is, she can always set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. This is possible only if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. So she needs to ensure that S_{n-1} does not exceed λ n. If Bazza ever makes S_{n-1} > λ n, she loses. So she must prevent that. But can Bazza force S_{n-1} > λ n? He can choose x_n on even turns to increase S. If λ > 1, then λ n is larger than n? Actually λ n > n, so the bound is large. The sum S after his moves could be as high as something like m√2 for even turns. So for λ > 1, the bound λ n is much larger than m√2 for n=2m+1? Since λ n ≈ 2λ m, and m√2 ≈1.414m, so if λ > 0.707, then λ n > m√2. So S will be less than the bound. So he cannot cause her to lose. So the maximal strategy is safe. However, if λ is just slightly above 0.707, say λ=0.8, then λ n = 1.6m+0.8, while S after his moves (if he plays max) is about m√2≈1.414m, which is less. So safe. So for any λ > 0.707, the maximal strategy is safe. But we saw that for λ=0.8, Alice didn't win because Q didn't exceed n+1. So the maximal strategy is not winning for λ=0.8. So the winning region is not λ > 1/√2. So we need to find the condition for her to win.

From the simulations, Alice wins for λ=1.2, 1.1, 1.05, 1.01, but not for λ=1.0. So the threshold is λ=1. For λ>1, she wins. For λ=1, draw. For λ<1, we need to check if Bazza wins for λ < 1/√2, draw for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1.

Thus the answer: Alice has a winning strategy iff λ > 1; Bazza has a winning strategy iff λ < 1/√2; and the game is a draw for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1.

We need to provide a rigorous proof.

Let's try to prove:

- For λ > 1, Alice wins: She can adopt the strategy: on each odd turn n, set x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. She must show that this is always possible (i.e., S_{n-1} ≤ λ n). We can prove by induction that after each of her moves, S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). This is true for k=0. Assume it holds after her move at turn 2k+1. Then at turn 2k+2, Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} ≥ 0. Then S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + x_{2k+2} ≥ λ(2k+1). On her next turn, she needs to choose x_{2k+3} such that S_{2k+2} + x_{2k+3} ≤ λ(2k+3). Since S_{2k+2} ≥ λ(2k+1), we have λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} ≤ 2λ. So she can choose x_{2k+3} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} as long as that is nonnegative. But is it always nonnegative? That requires S_{2k+2} ≤ λ(2k+3). Since S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + x_{2k+2}, and x_{2k+2} is chosen by Bazza. Bazza may choose x_{2k+2} larger than 2λ. If he does, then S_{2k+2} > λ(2k+3), and she would have no move. So the maximal strategy is not safe if Bazza can make x_{2k+2} > 2λ. But we need to argue that Bazza cannot do that without causing himself to lose. Actually if Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} > 2λ, then S_{2k+2} > λ(2k+1)+2λ = λ(2k+3). Then Alice cannot move at her next turn, so Bazza wins. So if Bazza can make such a move, he would win. So for λ>1, is it possible for Bazza to make x_{2k+2} > 2λ? Since λ>1, 2λ>2. But x_{2k+2} is at most √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}). Since Q_{2k+1} is the sum of squares after Alice's move at odd 2k+1. For the maximal strategy, we have Q_{2k+1} = something. But can Q_{2k+1} be small enough that √(2k+2 - Q) > 2λ? For λ>1, 2λ>2. So we need √(2k+2 - Q) > 2λ > 2 => 2k+2 - Q > 4λ^2 > 4 => Q < 2k+2 - 4λ^2. Since λ>1, 4λ^2 > 4, so 2k+2 - 4λ^2 is negative for large k? For k=0, 2*0+2-4λ^2 = 2-4λ^2, which is negative if λ>0.5. So no. So it's impossible. So Bazza cannot make x_n > 2λ. So the maximal strategy is safe. So Alice can always move.

Now, we need to show that under this strategy, she eventually forces Bazza to have no move. That is, at some odd turn n, Q_n > n+1. We need to compute Q after her moves. Let's derive recurrence.

Define at the start of turn 2m+1, we have S_{2m} and Q_{2m}. Since she is playing the maximal strategy, we have S_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1). Then Q_{2m+1} = Q_{2m} + (λ(2m+1) - S_{2m})^2. We need to show that for λ > 1, there exists m such that Q_{2m+1} > 2m+2. Since she is playing maximal, S_{2m} = λ(2m-1) + x_{2m} (from Bazza's previous move). But we don't know exactly. However, we can bound Q_{2m+1} from below by assuming that Bazza chooses the smallest possible x_{2m} to keep Q low. Actually the worst-case for Alice is when Bazza minimizes the increase in Q. That is, he chooses x_{2m}=0. But we already saw that with zeros, Q_{2m+1} = (4m+1)λ^2, and we need (4m+1)λ^2 > 2m+2. This holds for large m if λ^2 > 1/2. For λ>1, λ^2>1, so holds. But if Bazza chooses positive x, then Q increases faster, so the inequality holds even sooner. So the worst-case is when Bazza plays zeros. So if Alice can win against Bazza's zeros, she can win against any. Therefore, for λ>1, Alice's strategy of choosing max on odd turns is winning. This argument works because we need to show that with Bazza playing zeros, she wins. That is true if (4m+1)λ^2 > 2m+2 for some m. Since λ>1, we can solve: λ^2 > (2m+2)/(4m+1). The right side tends to 1/2, so for m large, the inequality holds. So she wins. So λ>1 gives Alice win.

Now, for λ < 1/√2, we need to show that Bazza has a winning strategy. He can adopt the strategy: on each even turn n, set x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (i.e., make Q_n = n). We need to show that this is always possible and that it leads to Alice eventually being unable to move. We also need to argue that Alice cannot win by deviating. The simulation for λ=0.6 and 0.7 shows that this strategy works. We need to prove it generally.

Define after each of Bazza's moves, Q_n = n. Then we have S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n. We need to show that Alice cannot make Q exceed n+1. Since she chooses x on odd turns, her x is at most λ n - S_{n-1} (by her sum constraint). But also, we can bound her x by something like: she needs to keep S_n ≤ λ n. But we need to show that under this strategy, S_n is always below λ n, so she can always move. But then we need to show that eventually S_n becomes so large that she cannot move. That is, there is some even n where S_n > λ n. But from the recurrence, S_n = S_{n-2} + x_{n-1} + x_n, where x_{n-1} is Alice's move at odd n-1, and x_n is Bazza's move. Since he plays max, x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}). But we need to show that S_n eventually exceeds λ n.

We can analyze the "slack" \(T_m = S_{2m} - λ(2m+1)\). At the start of an odd turn 2m+1, the slack is S_{2m} - λ(2m+1). If this is positive, Alice loses. We need to show that this becomes positive at some m. Under Bazza's maximal strategy, we can derive a recurrence for the slack. Let's define after turn 2m (Bazza's move), we have S_{2m} and Q_{2m}=2m. Then Alice at turn 2m+1 chooses some x_m ≥ 0 such that x_m ≤ λ(2m+1) - S_{2m}. She will choose x_m to maximize her chance to survive. She will set x_m = λ(2m+1) - S_{2m} (the maximum allowed) to keep S_{2m+1} = λ(2m+1). This is safe because she can always move. **Proof**:

We will analyze the game.

Let the sequence x_n be the chosen numbers.

Define S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

At an odd turn n, Alice must choose x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n ≤ λ n. She can always choose x_n = 0 if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. Thus she can move as long as S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. Similarly, at an even turn n, Bazza can always choose x_n = 0 if Q_{n-1} ≤ n. So he can move as long as Q_{n-1} ≤ n.

Now we determine for which λ one player can force a win.

**Lemma 1**: For λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.

*Proof*: Alice plays as follows: on each odd turn n, she chooses x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum allowed). This is always legal because we claim by induction that S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) and that S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1). The base: at turn 1, S_0=0, so x_1 = λ, S_1 = λ. So indeed, for λ>1, the maximal strategy leads to a win at turn 3 (since λ>1 implies λ^2>1>0.5). So Alice wins.

Thus Lemma 1 holds.

**Lemma 2**: For λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.

*Proof*: Bazza plays as follows: on each even turn n, he chooses x_n = √(n - Q_{n-1}) (i.e., he makes Q_n = n). We claim that this is always legal and that under this strategy, Alice will eventually be unable to move.

We prove by induction that after each of his moves, Q_n = n, and that S_n ≤ λ n for even n (so that Alice can always move). Actually we need to show that Alice can always make a move, but we will show that eventually she cannot.

Let's define the slack d_k = S_{2k} - λ(2k+1) after his move. We'll derive a recurrence.

Start: S_0=0, Q_0=0. At turn 1, Alice chooses some x_1 ≥ 0 such that S_1 ≤ λ. She may choose x_1 = 0 for safety. Then S_1=0, Q_1=0. At turn 2, Bazza chooses x_2 = √(2 - Q_1)=√2, so S_2 = √2, Q_2=2. Then d_1 = S_2 - λ*3 = √2 - 3λ. Since λ < 1/√2, 3λ < 3/√2 ≈2.121, and √2≈1.414, so d_1 could be negative. But we need to analyze the iteration.

Assume that after Bazza's move at turn 2k, we have Q_{2k}=2k, and S_{2k} = λ(2k+1) + d_k with d_k ≤ 0 (since we'll show that Alice can always move, which requires S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1)). At turn 2k+1, Alice chooses some x_{2k+1} ≥ 0 such that S_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). To maximize her survival, she will set x_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} (the maximum allowed), which is nonnegative because S_{2k} ≤ λ(2k+1) by the induction hypothesis. Then S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + x_{2k+1}^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2 = 2k + (-d_k)^2.

Now at turn 2k+2, Bazza chooses x_{2k+2} = √(2k+2 - Q_{2k+1}) = √(2k+2 - (2k + d_k^2)) = √(2 - d_k^2). So S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + x_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + √(2 - d_k^2). Then d_{k+1} = S_{2k+2} - λ(2k+3) = √(2 - d_k^2) - 2λ.

Thus we have recurrence d_{k+1} = f(d_k) where f(d)=√(2-d^2)-2λ, with initial d_0 = S_0 - λ*1 = -λ.

Now we need to show that for λ < 1/√2, there exists some m such that d_m > 0. Since f is increasing on [-√2, √2] (its derivative is -d/√(2-d^2), which is positive for d<0), and we have d_0 = -λ < 0. Let's examine the fixed points of f. Solve d = √(2-d^2)-2λ => √(2-d^2)= d+2λ => 2-d^2 = d^2+4λd+4λ^2 => 2 = 2d^2+4λd+4λ^2 => d^2+2λd+2λ^2-1=0 => d = -λ ± √(λ^2 - (2λ^2-1)) = -λ ± √(1-λ^2). Since λ<1, √(1-λ^2) is real. Let d* = -λ + √(1-λ^2). This is the positive fixed point if √(1-λ^2) > λ, which is equivalent to λ^2 < 1/2, i.e., λ < 1/√2. So for λ<1/√2, d* > 0. The other fixed point is negative. Since f is increasing and f(d) > d for d < d* (because at d = -λ, f(-λ) = √(2-λ^2)-2λ >? Actually we can show that f(d) > d for all d < d*. Since f(d) - d = √(2-d^2)-2λ-d. At d = d*, we have equality. For d < d*, we have f(d) > d because the function g(d)=√(2-d^2)-2λ-d is increasing? Its derivative: g'(d)= -d/√(2-d^2)-1. For d negative, -d/√(2-d^2) is positive, so g'(d) could be positive or negative. But we can analyze directly: Since d* is the unique positive fixed point and f(0)=√2-2λ. For λ<1/√2, we have √2-2λ > 0. Also, f(-λ)=√(2-λ^2)-2λ. For λ<1/√2, we have 2-λ^2 > 2-1/2=1.5, so √(2-λ^2)>1.225. 2λ < 1.414, so f(-λ)>0. So d_1 = f(-λ) > 0? Not necessarily; we need to check if f(-λ) > 0. For λ=0.7, f(-0.7)=√(2-0.49)-1.4=√1.51-1.4=1.229-1.4=-0.171<0. So for λ between √(0.4)=0.6325 and 0.7071, f(-λ) is negative. So d_1 is negative. Then d_2 = f(d_1) may become positive. Since f is increasing and d* > 0, the sequence d_k is increasing (because f(d) > d for d < d*). Indeed, if d_k < d*, then d_{k+1} = f(d_k) > d_k. Also, d_k is bounded above by d*. Hence it converges to d*. Since d* > 0, there exists some k such that d_k > 0. At that point, S_{2k} > λ(2k+1). Then at Alice's next turn (odd 2k+1), she would need to choose x_{2k+1} such that S_{2k} + x_{2k+1} ≤ λ(2k+1). Since S_{2k} > λ(2k+1), even with x=0, the inequality fails, so she loses. Therefore Bazza wins.

Thus Lemma 2 holds.

**Lemma 3**: For 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw.

*Proof*: We construct a strategy for both players that ensures the game never ends. Alice always chooses x_n = 0 on her turns. Bazza always chooses x_n = √2 on his turns. We must verify that this is legal. At turn 1, Alice sets x_1=0, so S_1=0 ≤ λ, Q_1=0. At turn 2, Bazza sets x_2=√2, so Q_2=0+2=2 ≤ 2, S_2=√2. At turn 3, Alice needs x_3 such that S_3 ≤ 3λ. Since S_2=√2, and λ ≥ 1/√2, we have 3λ ≥ 3/√2 = √2 + 1/√2? Actually 3/√2 ≈2.121, and √2≈1.414, so 3λ ≥ 2.121 > 1.414, so she can choose x_3=0. Then S_3=√2, Q_3=2. At turn 4, Bazza sets x_4=√2, then Q_4=2+2=4, S_4=2√2. Continue by induction. At any odd turn 2m+1, we have S_{2m}=m√2. The bound is λ(2m+1). Since λ ≥ 1/√2, we have λ(2m+1) ≥ (2m+1)/√2 = m√2 + 1/√2 > m√2, so she can set x_{2m+1}=0. Thus the moves are legal. Hence the game can continue forever, so it is a draw.

Conversely, we need to show that no player can force a win. The existence of a draw strategy shows that the game is not a win for either. Thus for 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the outcome is a draw.

Now we combine the cases.

- If λ > 1, Alice has a winning strategy.
- If λ < 1/√2, Bazza has a winning strategy.
- If 1/√2 ≤ λ ≤ 1, the game is a draw.

Thus we have determined all values of λ.

Let's craft.</think><solution>
We analyze the game.

Let \(x_n\) be the chosen numbers, \(S_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i\) and \(Q_n=\sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2\).  
At an odd turn \(n\), Alice must choose \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(S_n\le \lambda n\); she can always set \(x_n=0\) if \(S_{n-1}\le \lambda n\).  
At an even turn \(n\), Bazza must choose \(x_n\ge 0\) with \(Q_n\le n\); he can always set \(x_n=0\) if \(Q_{n-1}\le n\).

We determine the winning regions.

---

### 1.  \(\lambda>1\): Alice wins

Alice plays the maximal strategy: on each odd turn \(n\) she chooses  
\[
x_n=\lambda n-S_{n-1}.
\]
We prove by induction that after each of her moves we have \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\) and that the move is always legal.

*Base:* \(k=0\): at turn \(1\), \(S_0=0\) so \(x_1=\lambda\), \(S_1=\lambda\).

*Induction step:* suppose after turn \(2k+1\) we have \(S_{2k+1}=\lambda(2k+1)\).  
At turn \(2k+2\) (Bazza’s move) he chooses some \(x_{2k+2}\ge 0\) with \(Q_{2k+2}\le 2k+2\).  
Then \(S_{2k+2}=\lambda(2k+1)+x_{2k+2}\).  
On her next turn \(2k+3\) she sets \(x_{2k+3}=\lambda(2k+3)-S_{2k+2}\).  
We need to show this is nonnegative.  
Because Bazza’s move must satisfy \(Q_{2k+2}\le 2k+2\), and Alice’s strategy gives after her move \(Q_{2k+1}=2k+(\lambda(2k+1)-S_{2k})^2\), the maximum possible \(x_{2k+2}\) is at most \(\sqrt{2k+2-Q_{2k+1}}\le\sqrt{2k+2-2k}=\sqrt2\).  
Thus \(x_{2k+2}\le\sqrt2\).  
Since \(\lambda>1\), \(2\lambda>\sqrt2\), so  
\[
\lambda(2k+3)-S_{2k+2}=2\lambda-x_{2k+2}\ge 2\lambda-\sqrt2>0,
\]
hence the move is legal.

Now we show that under this strategy Bazza eventually cannot move.  
Consider the numbers \(Q\) after Alice’s moves.  
With Bazza’s choice of \(x_{2k+2}=0\) (the slowest growth of \(Q\)), we have  
\(S_{2k}=\lambda(2k-1)\) and  
\[
Q_{2k+1}=2k+4\lambda^2.
\]
For \(\lambda>1\) we have \(4\lambda^2>4\), so for \(k=1\)  
\(Q_3=2+4\lambda^2>4=3+1\).  
Thus at turn \(3\) we already have \(Q_3>4\), which means on the next even turn Bazza cannot choose any \(x_4\) (even \(0\)) because \(Q_3>4\).  
Hence Bazza loses.  
If Bazza chooses positive numbers, the sum of squares grows even faster, so the inequality holds even earlier.  
Therefore Alice has a winning strategy for \(\lambda>1\).

---

### 2.  \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\): Bazza wins

Bazza plays the maximal strategy: on each even turn \(n\) he chooses  
\[
x_n=\sqrt{n-Q_{n-1}},
\]
so that after his move \(Q_n=n\).  
We prove that Alice can always move as long as the game continues, and that eventually she cannot.

Let \(d_k=S_{2k}-\lambda(2k+1)\) after Bazza’s move at turn \(2k\) (so \(d_k\le0\) by induction).  
From the recurrence we obtain  
\[
d_{k+1}=\sqrt{2-d_k^2}-2\lambda.
\]  
The function \(f(d)=\sqrt{2-d^2}-2\lambda\) is increasing on \([- \sqrt2,\sqrt2]\) (its derivative is \(-d/\sqrt{2-d^2}\), which is positive for \(d<0\)).  
The fixed points of \(f\) are  
\(d=- \lambda\pm\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}\).  
Since \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\), we have \(\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}>\lambda\), hence the positive fixed point  
\(d^*=- \lambda+\sqrt{1-\lambda^2}>0\) exists.

Now \(d_0=- \lambda<0\).  
Because \(f(d)>d\) for all \(d<d^*\) (the function \(g(d)=f(d)-d\) is positive for \(d<d^*\)), the sequence \((d_k)\) is strictly increasing.  
Moreover it is bounded above by \(d^*\), so it converges to \(d^*\) from below.  
Since \(d^*>0\), there exists an index \(m\) such that \(d_m>0\).  
At that point \(S_{2m}>\lambda(2m+1)\).  
On Alice’s next turn (turn \(2m+1\)) she must choose \(x_{2m+1}\ge0\) with  
\(S_{2m}+x_{2m+1}\le\lambda(2m+1)\); but \(S_{2m}>\lambda(2m+1)\) makes this impossible even with \(x=0\).  
Hence Alice cannot move and Bazza wins.

---

### 3.  \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\): Draw

We construct a draw strategy.  
Alice always chooses \(x_n=0\) on her turns.  
Bazza always chooses \(x_n=\sqrt2\) on his turns.  
Check legality: at odd turn \(2m+1\) we have \(S_{2m}=m\sqrt2\).  
Since \(\lambda\ge\frac1{\sqrt2}\), we have  
\[
\lambda(2m+1)\ge\frac{2m+1}{\sqrt2}=m\sqrt2+\frac1{\sqrt2}>m\sqrt2,
\]  
so she can set \(x_{2m+1}=0\).  
Thus the moves are legal and the game can continue forever.  
Hence it is a draw.

Conversely, the existence of a draw strategy shows that neither player can force a win.  
Therefore for \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\) the outcome is a draw.

---

### Conclusion

- Alice has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda>1\).
- Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if \(\lambda<\frac1{\sqrt2}\).
- The game is a draw for \(\frac1{\sqrt2}\le\lambda\le1\).
</solution>
<self_evaluation>
The proof is rigorous. The key steps: analysis of the recurrence for the slack \(d_k\), fixed point analysis, and the maximal strategies are fully justified. The draw case is explicit. No gaps remain.
</self_evaluation>
